PREFACE.

IVM-liaiis  no  ai)()k\u\-  is  inTtlcd  for  iiiti'odueing  tho  present  work  to
the  notice  of  oriental  ricliolar.s  in  general  and  Arabic  scholars  in
i)articnlar.  Having  been  interested  for  many  years  in  the  colloqnial
Arabic  of  Egypt,  the  compiler  was  strnck  -  as,  no  donbt,  all
those  who  have  studied  colloquial  Arabic  must  also  have  been-by  the
wealth  of  the  dialect,  not  only  in  words  but  informs  of  expression  and
subtle  meanings,  and  the  more  he  studied  the  subject,  the  more  he
liccanie  convinced  of  the  necessit\-  and  utility  of  a  vocabulary  to
introduce  the  foreigner  to  the  still  uncomprehended  treasures  of
colloquial  Arabic.

But  it  was  not  until  ISDl  that  he  began  to  collect  his  materials.
His  plan  at  first  was  to  conq)ile  a  simple  book  of  some  two  hundred
ijiiges,  but  as  the  work  progressed  he  foimd  that  it  was  gradually
o-rowing  larger  until  at  last  its  size  as  originally  contemplated  was
ijreatly  increased.  Finding  this  to  be  the  case,  the  compiler  decided
to  "ivc  the  book  a  wider  scope,  and  for  this  purpose  he  introduced  a
large  number  of  administrative,  financial,  engineering,  mechanical  and
military  words  which  he  met  with  in  his  perusal  of  a  mass  of  official
publications.  The  idioms  and  the  slang  phrases  Avere  obtained  not  only
l)y  reading  almost  all  the  literature  of  which  colloquial  Arabic  can
boast,  but  by  listening  and  carefully  noting  casual  words  and  trivial
phrases   as  they  were  used  in  connnon  conversation.

In  the  early  part  of  the  year  the  book  was  ready  for  the  i)n'ss,  but
it  must  not  for  a  moment  be  imagined  that  all  the  phrases  and  charac-
teristic expressions  in  use  amongst  the  natives  of  this  country  have
been  given.  No  single  comjjiler  can  hope  to  make  an  exhaustive
collection,  lint  it  can  be  confidently  stated  that  the  material  here
collected  represents  almost  all  that  a  foreigner  wouhl  nu'ct  with  in
conversation    with  tiie    natives  of  Egypt.

(Jreat  difficult}-  was  oltcn  met  witli  in  ascertaining  the  precise
meaning  of  some  words.  The  comi)iler  then  had  recourse  to  his
friends  and  in  certain  instances  conflicting  definitions  were  given.  Tlie
reason  is  not  f;ir  to  seek.  Collo([uial  Arabic  not  having  been  writti'u
until  ijuite  recently,  woi'ds  are  sometimes  used  as  the  fancy  ot  the
speaki'i-  dictatos.  fn  these  cases,  the  meanings  given  are  those  most  imi-
versally  accepted.  As  regards  the  idi(jms  and  the  slang,  the  conqiiler  did

his  best  to  obtain  the  equivalent  English  idioms,  and  where  this  was
not  possible,  an  expression  as  near  as  possible  resembling  tlie  original
was  given.  For  this  reason  many  of  the  English  idiomatic  and  other
expressions  can  be  improved  upon,  but  the  student  can  be  assured  that
no  pains  have  been  spared  to  render  the  book  accurate  and  trust-
worthy.

While  the  book  was  in  the  press  objections  were  made  to  the  de-
rivatives separately  given  and  to  the  translitei*ation.  Those  experienced
in  teaching  Arabic  to  foreigners  in  Egypt  know  that  only  a  small  per-
centage of  these  study  the  syntax  and  the  grammar  and  that  the
Egyptians  themselves  are  often  at  a  loss  to  find  a  word  in  a  purely
Arabic  lexicon  where  the  derivatives  are  given  only  under  the  root
verb.  As  tar  as  the  transliteration  is  concerned,  the  system  adopted  is
that  of  the  late  Spitta  Bey  -  pronounced  by  scholars  to  be  the  most
scientific  -  simplified  by  a  distinguished  American  scholar.  It  may  per-
ha])s  ])e  added  that  no  system  of  translitei-ation  is  perfect  and  the
adoption  of  system  is  a  matter  of  taste.

In  conclusion,  the  compiler  wishes  to  express  his  grateful  thanks
to  his  numerous  friends,  both  in  this  country  and  al)road,  for  tlieir
kind  assistance  and  encouragement  whicli  gave  him  fresh  vigour  at
moments  Avhen  he  felt  w'eary,  and  enabled  him  to  summon  up  courage
to  bring  his  work  to  a  conclusion.  He  wishes  that  it  were  possible
lor  him  to  name  them  all,  but  certain  considerations  prevent  his  doing
S(j.  Mention  must,  however,  ])e  made  of  I.  G.  Mussawir  Bey,  of  the
Piildic  Works  Department,  who  read  a  part  of  the  manuscript  making
valual)le  suggestions.

Thanks  are  also  due  to  the  printers  for  the  diligence  and  skill
with  which  they  have  executed  their  difficult  and  complicated  task,
submitting  to  numerous  alterations  and  corrections  whilst  it  was  in
the  press.

Cairo,  S.  S.

y^uvcmber  1895.

NOTE.

The  mnnner  which  the  compiler  has  adopted  in  giving  the  verbs
appears  to  hira  to  be  easier  than  that  hitherto  followed  by  Arabic
lexicographers.  Only  such  forms  and  derivatives  are  given  as  are  more
generally  used  by  the  natives.  The  present  tense,  given  in  parenthesis
after  the  past,  is  omitted  in  the  transliteration  when  it  is  formed  from
the  past  by  the  prefix  "//".  The  plurals,  the  formation  of  which  is
one  of  the  main  difficulties  of  the  language,  are  invariabl\-  yiven.
The  asterisk  denotes  that    the  word  is  fureiini.
